	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en"><i>Last Week Tonight with John Oliver</i></h1>

<p><i><b>Last Week Tonight with John Oliver</b></i> (often abridged as <i><b>Last Week Tonight</b></i>) is an American <a href="/wiki/Late-night_talk_show" title="Late-night talk show">late-night talk</a> and <a href="/wiki/News_satire" title="News satire">news satire</a> television program hosted by comedian <a href="/wiki/John_Oliver" title="John Oliver">John Oliver</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-order_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-order-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The half-hour-long show premiered on Sunday, April 27, 2014, on <a href="/wiki/HBO" title="HBO">HBO</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> <i>Last Week Tonight</i> shares some similarities with <a href="/wiki/Comedy_Central" title="Comedy Central">Comedy Central</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Daily_Show" title="The Daily Show">The Daily Show</a></i> (where Oliver was previously <a href="/wiki/List_of_The_Daily_Show_correspondents" title="List of The Daily Show correspondents">featured as a correspondent</a> and fill-in host), as it takes a satirical look at news, politics and current events, but on a weekly basis.<sup id="cite_ref-order_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-order-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Oliver has stated that he has "full <a href="/wiki/Artistic_freedom" title="Artistic freedom">creative freedom</a>, including free rein to <a href="/wiki/Criticisms_of_corporations" title="Criticisms of corporations">criticize corporations</a>". His initial contract with HBO was for two years with an option for extension.<sup id="cite_ref-freedom_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-freedom-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> In September 2017, HBO announced that the show had been renewed for three additional seasons of 30 episodes each, keeping the show on the air through 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The show's seventh season, the last season promised by the 2017 renewal announcement, premiered on February 16, 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-S6_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S6-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Last_Week_Tonight_with_John_Oliver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Oliver described his preparations for the show to an interviewer for <i><a href="/wiki/The_Atlantic#The_Wire" title="The Atlantic">The Wire</a></i>: "... I basically have to watch everything. The only thing I kind of watch for pleasure is <a href="/wiki/Fareed_Zakaria_GPS" title="Fareed Zakaria GPS">Fareed Zakaria's show</a> on Sundays ... That and <i><a href="/wiki/60_Minutes" title="60 Minutes">60 Minutes</a></i> I watch for pleasure, or maybe <i><a href="/wiki/Frontline_(American_TV_program)" title="Frontline (American TV program)">Frontline</a></i> ... I have a TV on in my office all the time and I'll generally flick around on that from <a href="/wiki/CNN" title="CNN">CNN</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fox_News" title="Fox News">Fox</a>, <a href="/wiki/MSNBC" title="MSNBC">MSNBC</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bloomberg_Television" title="Bloomberg Television">Bloomberg</a>, <a href="/wiki/CNBC" title="CNBC">CNBC</a>, <a href="/wiki/Al_Jazeera_America" title="Al Jazeera America">Al Jazeera</a> ... I'm watching with a certain thing in mind and that is how to see a story told badly."<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>He said to another interviewer that he is concerned about dealing with <a href="/wiki/News_values" title="News values">old news</a>:
</p>
<blockquote><p>If something happens on a Monday, realistically all the meat is going to be picked off that bone by the time it gets to us&#160;— there's probably barely a point in doing it ... I think we'll be attracted to some extent by stories that are off the grid ... Our show may end up skewing more international in terms of stories.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Tim_Carvell" title="Tim Carvell">Tim Carvell</a>, executive producer of <i>Last Week Tonight</i>, explained in an interview how the cast and crew deal with a half hour of Oliver speaking without any <a href="/wiki/Television_advertisement" title="Television advertisement">commercial</a> breaks.<sup id="cite_ref-usnews_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usnews-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Structural considerations are leading to changes in the content in the show that will inherently make it different from <i>The Daily Show</i> ... We realized early on, you don't necessarily want to hear anybody talk to you for a half an hour straight&#160;– even John, who is very charming&#160;– so we are constructing these little, produced comedy elements that will serve the function of commercial breaks throughout the show, which will let us get out of the studio, get us away from John's voice and break the show up a bit.
</p></blockquote>
<p>Carvell also revealed that HBO gave them freedom in choosing guests for the show, advising them not to feel obligated to feature celebrities.<sup id="cite_ref-usnews_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usnews-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> When asked by an interviewer about "<a href="/wiki/Correspondent" title="Correspondent">correspondents</a>" such as those featured on <i>The Daily Show</i>, Oliver replied, "we're not going to be a parody news show, so no people pretending to be journalists."<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Format">Format</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Last_Week_Tonight_with_John_Oliver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Format">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="box-More_citations_needed_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Last_Week_Tonight_with_John_Oliver&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.<br /><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22Last+Week+Tonight+with+John+Oliver%22">"Last Week Tonight with John Oliver"</a>&#160;–&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22Last+Week+Tonight+with+John+Oliver%22+-wikipedia">news</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22Last+Week+Tonight+with+John+Oliver%22+site:news.google.com/newspapers&amp;source=newspapers">newspapers</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22Last+Week+Tonight+with+John+Oliver%22+-wikipedia">books</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22Last+Week+Tonight+with+John+Oliver%22">scholar</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22Last+Week+Tonight+with+John+Oliver%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on">JSTOR</a></span></small></span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">August 2015</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The format is similar to <i><a href="/wiki/The_Daily_Show" title="The Daily Show">The Daily Show</a></i>, with Oliver sitting at a desk in front of a backdrop of a skyline containing buildings from around the world, often relating to the current topic. Unlike <i>The Daily Show</i>, Oliver tends to explore one concept in depth but retains the appearance of commercial breaks to intercut larger topics with smaller segments that are not necessarily related to the topic at hand. The show is taped in front of a <a href="/wiki/Studio_audience" title="Studio audience">studio audience</a>, and HBO offers a limited number of free tickets to attend each week's <i>Last Week Tonight</i> taping.
</p><p>The show's theme starts off every episode containing images relating to it or the world at large with satirical captions written in <a href="/wiki/Dog_Latin" title="Dog Latin">dog Latin</a>. Each episode covers a small handful of shorter segments, and then one main segment. While the short segments almost always relate to recent news, the episode's main segment usually covers in length and detail a political issue, even if that issue did not have news media attention during the preceding week. On occasion, the short segments are omitted and the main segment takes up the entirety of the episode.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Oliver injects humor into his presentation, including satirical <a href="/wiki/Analogies" class="mw-redirect" title="Analogies">analogies</a>, and allusions to popular culture and celebrities. The show includes a panel in the upper-left corner that frequently displays a photo or graphic for that accompanies subject at hand, which aids in the humor. A full-screen graphic will show and play a video clip (such as excerpts from news show or documentaries) when Oliver is citing it.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> He often coins <a href="/wiki/Hashtag" title="Hashtag">hashtags</a> related to his segment for use in <a href="/wiki/Social_media" title="Social media">social media</a>, some of which go <a href="/wiki/Viral_phenomenon" title="Viral phenomenon">viral</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Emertainment_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emertainment-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A recurring element of the show is Oliver's use of mascots. The mascots used in the show include Jeff the Diseased Lung in a Cowboy Hat, Hoots the NSA Owl, Taryn the Tinder Chicken, and the Last Week Tonight puppets.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Oliver told Vulture in February 2019, "I mean, the truth of the mascots is that we have a gigantic room filled with spectacular mascot costumes now. [Executive producer] Tim Carvell and I — I think we’ve always seen the show as our attempt to make The Muppet Show and failing to do so. But occasionally, just occasionally, you get the kind of Muppet Show adoration in the ludicrous mascots."<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The typical structure of the show is to open with a recap of a few of the week's news stories, segue into a video compilation, and then move on to Oliver's main segment. Some of the episodes will follow up the main segment with another video compilation or news story. Oliver's speech is broken up with video compilations of recent news clips, or recurring segments, into which Oliver segues with the slogan, "And now, this." Oliver has also ended some segments with mock trailers for fictional TV shows or commercials that satirize the subject of his speech.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Celebrity_guests">Celebrity guests</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Last_Week_Tonight_with_John_Oliver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Celebrity guests">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The show frequently features one or more celebrity guests in its segments to help Oliver better get his point on the week's topic across. Guests have featured in many different capacities including giving monologues directly to camera, being interviewed by Oliver, playing musical numbers, participating in comedic skits with or without Oliver, or starring in fictitious parody <a href="/wiki/Public_service_announcement" title="Public service announcement">PSAs</a>. On the show, guests are sometimes seen in the Last Week Tonight studio itself doing their part during the show's main taping, while at other times they are seen in video clips that were pre-recorded at a different location and later included in the show. Oliver often interviews personalities who were directly involved in or impacted by the issues being discussed in the show to get their first-hand experiences and viewpoints on the issue. Some of the famous personalities Oliver has interviewed include <a href="/wiki/Edward_Snowden" title="Edward Snowden">Edward Snowden</a>, the <a href="/wiki/14th_Dalai_Lama" title="14th Dalai Lama">14th</a> <a href="/wiki/Dalai_Lama" title="Dalai Lama">Dalai Lama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anita_Hill" title="Anita Hill">Professor Anita Hill</a> and <a href="/wiki/Monica_Lewinsky" title="Monica Lewinsky">Monica Lewinsky</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Episodes">Episodes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Last_Week_Tonight_with_John_Oliver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Episodes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="YouTube_channel">YouTube channel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Last_Week_Tonight_with_John_Oliver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: YouTube channel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><i>Last Week Tonight</i> has a <a href="/wiki/YouTube" title="YouTube">YouTube</a> channel where videos are intermittently (depending on series) added after a show has aired.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Most of the videos are of the main segment from each episode. Some extended clips are also available on the show's YouTube channel, as are "Web Exclusives" that are produced solely for the channel, such as a July 2016 video in which Oliver responded to negative comments left on the channel itself.<sup id="cite_ref-Emertainment_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emertainment-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The show's production has also created content specifically for fan use. For the March 19, 2017, episode, which reported on Bolivia's growing coalition of workers clad in zebra suits to educate civilians about traffic laws, the show's production recorded 23 minutes of a person in a zebra costume dancing and gesticulating before a green screen so that viewers could edit it into other videos for humorous effect.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> A similar video called "Real Animals, Fake Paws" for use in reenacting <a href="/wiki/U.S._Supreme_Court" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Supreme Court">U.S. Supreme Court</a> cases was released after the October 19, 2014, episode where dogs were used instead of justices to make listening to oral court cases more amusing to the general public and to increase interest in the subject.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By April 2015, the channel had attained over a million subscribers.<sup id="cite_ref-Emertainment_15-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emertainment-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> That number had risen to 3.6 million by July 2016,<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> and over 5 million by May 2017. As of September&#160;2019<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Last_Week_Tonight_with_John_Oliver&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, the channel had over 7.3 million subscribers and more than 2.1 billion views.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_media">Social media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Last_Week_Tonight_with_John_Oliver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Social media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><i>Last Week Tonight</i> has a Facebook page with over 2.6 million likes, a Twitter account with over 2.9 million followers, and an Instagram account with more than 450,000 followers as of July&#160;2019<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Last_Week_Tonight_with_John_Oliver&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>.
</p><p>Oliver often makes use of joke <a href="/wiki/Hashtags" class="mw-redirect" title="Hashtags">hashtags</a> to facilitate the <a href="/wiki/Viral_spread" class="mw-redirect" title="Viral spread">viral spread</a> of his videos on <a href="/wiki/Twitter" title="Twitter">Twitter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Facebook" title="Facebook">Facebook</a>, as with the hashtag #NotMyChristian, which he used in 2014 to complain about the casting of actor <a href="/wiki/Jamie_Dornan" title="Jamie Dornan">Jamie Dornan</a> in the feature film adaptation of <i><a href="/wiki/Fifty_Shades_of_Grey_(film)" title="Fifty Shades of Grey (film)">Fifty Shades of Grey</a></i>. The hashtag became a recurring punchline on the show, mentioned in both serious stories and casual segues.<sup id="cite_ref-Emertainment_15-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emertainment-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reception">Reception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Last_Week_Tonight_with_John_Oliver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Reception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:207px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Oliver_2014.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/John_Oliver_2014.jpg/205px-John_Oliver_2014.jpg" decoding="async" width="205" height="250" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/John_Oliver_2014.jpg/307px-John_Oliver_2014.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/John_Oliver_2014.jpg/410px-John_Oliver_2014.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1160" data-file-height="1415" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Oliver_2014.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>John Oliver, host of <i>Last Week Tonight</i></div></div></div>
<p>Oliver's debut show garnered 1.11 million viewers. The number of viewers online, through websites such as YouTube showing extended clips of different segments, have steadily climbed into multiple millions. The show's YouTube channel also features Web Exclusives which are occasionally posted when the main show is taking a week off. Across the TV airings, <a href="/wiki/Digital_video_recorder" title="Digital video recorder">DVR</a>, <a href="/wiki/Video_on_demand" title="Video on demand">on-demand</a> and <a href="/wiki/HBO_Go" title="HBO Go">HBO Go</a>, <i>Last Week Tonight</i> averaged 4.1 million weekly viewers in its first season.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Last Week Tonight</i> has received widespread critical acclaim. Matthew Jacobs of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Huffington_Post" class="mw-redirect" title="The Huffington Post">The Huffington Post</a></i> named Oliver's program as 2014's best television show, writing "the year's most surprising contribution to television is a show that bucked conventional formats, left us buzzing and paved the way for a burgeoning dynasty. <i>Last Week Tonight with John Oliver</i> is 2014's crowning achievement."<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Hank_Stuever" title="Hank Stuever">Hank Stuever</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">The Washington Post</a></i> compared Oliver's program with <i>The Daily Show</i> several times in his review of Oliver's debut:
</p>
<blockquote><p>Another scathing, stick-it-to-'em critique of American <a href="/wiki/Mass_media" title="Mass media">mass media</a> and politics shellacked in satire and delivered by a funny if almost off-puttingly incredulous man with a British accent ... Exactly like <i>The Daily Show</i>, the goal is to make elected and appointed officials, as well as just about any corporate enterprise, look foolish and inept while slyly culling together television news clips that make the media look equally inept at covering such evident truths.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/James_Poniewozik" title="James Poniewozik">James Poniewozik</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> similarly compared <i>Last Week</i> with <i>The Daily Show</i>, but also wrote that the "full half-hour gives Oliver the room to do more," and praised Oliver's "sharper tone and his globalist, English-outsider perspective," as well as his "genuine passion over his subjects." Poniewozik wrote that Oliver's debut was "a funny, confident start."<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Entertainment_Weekly" title="Entertainment Weekly">Entertainment Weekly</a></i> review began by ringing the same changes: "The fear with <i>Last Week Tonight</i> is that it's <i>The Daily Show</i> except once a week&#160;— a staggered timeline that would rob the basic news-punning format of its intrinsic topical punch ... The first episode of his HBO series didn't stray far from the <a href="/wiki/Jon_Stewart" title="Jon Stewart">[Jon] Stewart</a> mothership, stylistically ..." However, the reviewer, Darren Franich, liked that Oliver has "a half-hour of television that is simultaneously tighter and more ambitious, that the extra production time leads to sharper gags but also the ability to present more context" and thought that the debut had "plenty of funny throwaway lines." Franich appreciated Oliver's coverage of <a href="/wiki/2014_Indian_general_election" title="2014 Indian general election">the 2014 Indian election</a>, which the American press was largely ignoring,<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> and, like Poniewozik, praised Oliver's "passion." Franich concluded that <i>Last Week Tonight</i> "suggested the sharpest possible version of its inspiration" and that it "should feel like an experiment" but "felt almost fully formed."<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>David Haglund of <i><a href="/wiki/Slate_(magazine)" title="Slate (magazine)">Slate</a></i> was ambivalent, writing that the show is "obviously a work in progress" and that one segment "felt like misplaced overkill," but also that it is "good use of a weekly show, and it was funny to boot."<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gawker" title="Gawker">Gawker</a>'s Jordan Sargent claimed <i>Last Week Tonight</i> was "the new <i>Daily Show</i>,"<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> while simultaneously criticizing the <i>Daily Show</i> for abandoning those "who have moved on from caring about <a href="/wiki/Fox_News" title="Fox News">Fox [News]</a> and <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Republican Party of the United States">Republicans</a>."
</p><p>A number of commentators from mainstream media outlets, including <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> <i>The Huffington Post</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Associated_Press" title="Associated Press">Associated Press</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> have described Oliver's style of reporting as journalism or even investigative journalism. Oliver himself disagrees, stating that "it's not journalism, it's comedy—it's comedy first, and it's comedy second."<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reaction_and_influence">Reaction and influence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Last_Week_Tonight_with_John_Oliver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Reaction and influence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Net_Neutrality_(Last_Week_Tonight)#&quot;Net_Neutrality&quot;" title="Net Neutrality (Last Week Tonight)">June 2014 segment</a> about <a href="/wiki/Net_neutrality_in_the_United_States" title="Net neutrality in the United States">net neutrality in the United States</a> was thought to spur over 45,000 comments on the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Communications_Commission" title="Federal Communications Commission">Federal Communications Commission</a>'s electronic filing page about a net neutrality proposal that, if implemented, would have priority "lanes" for certain internet traffic. The FCC also received an extra 300,000 comments in an email inbox designated specifically for the proposal.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> It was thought that Oliver's segment had a major role in the FCC's ultimate reversal of stance on that proposal,<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> with the FCC instead implementing net-neutrality rules that prohibited priority "lanes".<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> A sequel in 2017 inspired over 150,000 comments on a subsequent proposal to scrap the new net neutrality rules.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A segment on the then <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Australia" title="Prime Minister of Australia">Prime Minister of Australia</a> <a href="/wiki/Tony_Abbott" title="Tony Abbott">Tony Abbott</a> received widespread attention in Australia across the mainstream media and was trending on <a href="/wiki/Social_media" title="Social media">social media</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to a document obtained by <i><a href="/wiki/Vice_(magazine)" title="Vice (magazine)">Vice</a></i>, the military government of <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a> listed Oliver as "<a href="/wiki/L%C3%A8se_majest%C3%A9_in_Thailand" title="Lèse majesté in Thailand">undermining the royal institution</a>" for calling Crown Prince <a href="/wiki/Vajiralongkorn" title="Vajiralongkorn">Vajiralongkorn</a> a "buffoon" and an "idiot."<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The show also made international headlines following Oliver's interview with <a href="/wiki/Edward_Snowden" title="Edward Snowden">Edward Snowden</a>, which included a graphic, in-depth conversation about the amount of power the <a href="/wiki/United_States_government" class="mw-redirect" title="United States government">United States government</a> has at its disposal in terms of intelligence, both domestic and foreign. Oliver also confronted Snowden about the lack of knowledge of the American people about his work and why they may be hesitant to analyze it for themselves rather than accept preconceived notions of him being a <a href="/wiki/Whistleblower" title="Whistleblower">whistleblower</a>. Notably, he tried to help Snowden in creating public awareness for the fundamentality of the surveillance problem in putting forward the question "Can they see my dick?"<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In a segment about <a href="/wiki/Public_defender" title="Public defender">public defenders</a> and how some offices are extremely underfunded, the New Orleans Public Defense office's crowdfunding efforts to improve their conditions were featured. In the days following the episode's broadcast, thousands of dollars were donated to the office by the show's fans, helping them raise their goal four days after the show aired.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For a segment in the October 18, 2015, broadcast, Oliver put on a comically grand demonstration with Canadian actor <a href="/wiki/Mike_Myers" title="Mike Myers">Mike Myers</a> to urge Canadian voters to vote against <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Harper" title="Stephen Harper">Stephen Harper</a> in the next day's <a href="/wiki/2015_Canadian_federal_election" title="2015 Canadian federal election">Canadian federal election</a>. As part of this gesture, Oliver displayed $5000 in cash in anticipation of being charged for the crime of being a foreigner attempting to induce Canadian citizens to change their vote as per section 331 of the <a href="/wiki/Canada_Elections_Act" title="Canada Elections Act">Canada Elections Act</a>. However, the agency responsible for overseeing federal elections in Canada, <a href="/wiki/Elections_Canada" title="Elections Canada">Elections Canada</a>, explained the next day that prosecution of Oliver will not be necessary since inducement in the act is defined as offering something material to voters. Per Elections Canada, Oliver has not tried to influence Canadian electorate, since the money shown was only for the anticipated fine, but merely expressed his opinion as a bystander, which is lawful.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the June 17, 2018 episode, Oliver spoke at length about <a href="/wiki/Xi_Jinping" title="Xi Jinping">Xi Jinping</a>, <a href="/wiki/General_Secretary_of_the_Communist_Party_of_China" title="General Secretary of the Communist Party of China">General Secretary of the Communist Party of China</a>, in part criticizing his <a href="/wiki/Censorship_in_China" title="Censorship in China">censorship</a> of Chinese media, his dictatorship<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> and consolidation of power.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Both "John Oliver" and "Last Week Tonight" were blocked from Chinese social media platform <a href="/wiki/Sina_Weibo" title="Sina Weibo">Sina Weibo</a> immediately following the segment.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/HBO" title="HBO">HBO</a> website and content from the network were also blocked,<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> although Last Week Tonight had already been excluded from <a href="/wiki/HBO_(Asia)" title="HBO (Asia)">HBO Asia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The promotion for Season 3 highlighted four derogatory quotes either the show or Oliver himself received: "Comedian fool" (former <a href="/wiki/FIFA" title="FIFA">FIFA</a> vice-president <a href="/wiki/Jack_Warner_(football_executive)" title="Jack Warner (football executive)">Jack Warner</a>, responding to a segment on the <a href="/wiki/2015_FIFA_corruption_case" title="2015 FIFA corruption case">2015 FIFA corruption case</a>), "Makes people dumb" (an article <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wall_Street_Journal" title="The Wall Street Journal">The Wall Street Journal</a></i> wrote about the show), "Very boring" (<a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a>'s assessment of the show), and "More unpleasant than a diuretic" (what <a href="/wiki/President_of_Ecuador" title="President of Ecuador">President of Ecuador</a> <a href="/wiki/Rafael_Correa" title="Rafael Correa">Rafael Correa</a> said "gringo talk shows" were like, after being featured in a segment).<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_John_Oliver_Effect">The John Oliver Effect</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Last_Week_Tonight_with_John_Oliver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: The John Oliver Effect">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/John_Oliver#Influence_and_the_&quot;John_Oliver_effect&quot;" title="John Oliver">John Oliver (Influence)</a></div>
<p>On several occasions, show segments on major societal issues, such as the "Chickens" or "Bail" segments, were soon followed by real-world change and action on said issues by the public, policymakers and/or other institutions. Several media outlets, noticing this pattern, suggested that attention from the show had instigated these changes, going so far as to dub the phenomenon the "John Oliver Effect".<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> One of the most commonly cited examples of this is Oliver's first Net Neutrality segment which allegedly led to more than 45,000 comments being sent to the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Communications_Commission" title="Federal Communications Commission">FCC</a>, crashing the website set up to receive comments in the process.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Oliver himself does not believe the effect to be real and has openly ridiculed the idea on the show, even calling the term "completely meaningless".
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tobacco">Tobacco</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Last_Week_Tonight_with_John_Oliver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Tobacco">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tobacco_(Last_Week_Tonight)" title="Tobacco (Last Week Tonight)">Tobacco (Last Week Tonight)</a></div>
<p>"Tobacco" is a segment about the tobacco industry, which aired on February 15, 2015, as part of the second episode of the second season. During the eighteen-minute segment, Oliver discusses tobacco industry trends and practices. He also introduces Jeff the Diseased Lung, a mascot he created for the American global cigarette and tobacco company <a href="/wiki/Philip_Morris_International" title="Philip Morris International">Philip Morris International</a>, the makers of <a href="/wiki/Marlboro_(cigarette)" title="Marlboro (cigarette)">Marlboro</a> brand of cigarettes. The anthropomorphic diseased lung, who smokes and coughs, has been compared to Joe Camel and the <a href="/wiki/Marlboro_Man" title="Marlboro Man">Marlboro Man</a>. Oliver and his team promoted the cartoon character by sending shirts with Jeff's image to <a href="/wiki/Togo" title="Togo">Togo</a> and displaying billboards in <a href="/wiki/Uruguay" title="Uruguay">Uruguay</a>, and by encouraging use of the hashtag #JeffWeCan, which trended on Twitter following the broadcast.
</p><p>The segment received widespread media coverage, with several outlets praising Oliver's ability to launch successful marketing campaigns and change perceptions about smoking through the creation of the mascot. The mascot later made an appearance at a protest organized by the "Campaign for Tobacco-Free Kids" in New York City in May 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Philip Morris International issued a response to the segment, stating that it included "many mischaracterizations" of the company.<sup id="cite_ref-Parker_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Parker-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Stranger_Than_Truth:_John_Oliver.27s_101_Favorite_History_Lies"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Stranger_Than_Truth:_John_Oliver's_101_Favorite_History_Lies">Stranger Than Truth: John Oliver's 101 Favorite History Lies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Last_Week_Tonight_with_John_Oliver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Stranger Than Truth: John Oliver&#039;s 101 Favorite History Lies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the "<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=pDVmldTurqk">Mandatory Minimums</a>" segment that aired on July 25, 2015, John Oliver talked about the controversial history of presidential pardoning, claiming that Abraham Lincoln once pardoned a man convicted of bestiality because he was intoxicated at the time; the man in question being John Wilkes Booth. He then confessed the statement was not true and for the first time mentioned a book compiling his favorite history lies.
</p><p>A couple of weeks later, he published a web-exclusive video <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=ACwenVzN2oU&amp;t=1s">("History Lies")</a> based on that reference, expanding the content for a book called "Stranger Than Truth: John Oliver's 101 Favorite History Lies", to be released in the following spring. At the end of the mini-segment, however, he confesses again that the entire book was also a lie.
</p><p>In what seems a continuation of the lie saga, the book surprisingly appeared on <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.amazon.co.uk/Stranger-Than-Truth-Olivers-Favorite/dp/0464820200/ref=sr_1_1?ie=UTF8&amp;qid=1543421965&amp;sr=8-1&amp;keywords=stranger+than+truth+john+oliver">Amazon UK</a> in October 2018.
</p><p>
The book description reads as follow: </p><blockquote><p>There is absolutely nothing more fun than lying confidently about history. Lying is incredibly fun. In fact, the only thing that feels better than lying to someone is lying to someone, then regaining their trust, and then lying to them again. This book, for example, was promised to be released in April 2016. But that was a lie. The plan was always October 2018. Because, you know, now is a time in history when lying openly gets you elected to office. </p></blockquote><blockquote><p>In this book, you will find plenty of plausible but completely unverifiable nonsense. Almost like watching Fox News but with a better sense of humour. Hopefully, it will teach you much about nothing other than the true joy of lying to people. Honestly, there has never been a better moment in history to be a liar. We have reached a point where truths have become lies and lies are now the truth. So learn from the greatest and get inspired by the best lies in history. </p></blockquote><blockquote><p>*NB: Most profits from this book are going to finance railway infrastructure under the English Channel. Contains strong language.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Our_Lady_of_Perpetual_Exemption">Our Lady of Perpetual Exemption</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Last_Week_Tonight_with_John_Oliver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Our Lady of Perpetual Exemption">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Our_Lady_of_Perpetual_Exemption" title="Our Lady of Perpetual Exemption">Our Lady of Perpetual Exemption</a></div>
<p>In August 2015, after delivering his "Televangelists" segment, Oliver hired a professional tax lawyer to set up a church called <a href="/wiki/Our_Lady_of_Perpetual_Exemption" title="Our Lady of Perpetual Exemption">Our Lady of Perpetual Exemption</a> as a legal entity. He did this partly as a way to demonstrate how "disturbingly easy" it is, in terms of paperwork, to set up a <a href="/wiki/Tax_exemption" title="Tax exemption">tax-exempt</a> religious organization as viewed by the <a href="/wiki/Internal_Revenue_Service" title="Internal Revenue Service">Internal Revenue Service</a>. As Oliver explained, the requirements needed to be defined as a "church" are quite broad. Since regulatory guidelines require an established location for a church, Oliver chose his studio location in New York City as its official location,<sup id="cite_ref-Mediaite_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mediaite-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> although he registered the <a href="/wiki/Nonprofit_organization" title="Nonprofit organization">nonprofit organization</a> in the state of <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Oliver's "megachurch" had a <a href="/wiki/Toll-free_telephone_number" title="Toll-free telephone number">toll-free phone number</a> which allowed callers to donate to the church, and said that any money collected would be redistributed to the charitable relief organization <a href="/wiki/Doctors_Without_Borders" class="mw-redirect" title="Doctors Without Borders">Doctors Without Borders</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-usatoday_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usatoday-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pedestrian_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pedestrian-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> Oliver announced the formation of his church on the episode of the show that aired on August 16, 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-TimeMag_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TimeMag-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Matt Wilstein, writing for <i><a href="/wiki/Mediaite" title="Mediaite">Mediaite</a></i>, saw Oliver's stunt as being along the same lines as comedian <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Colbert" title="Stephen Colbert">Stephen Colbert</a>'s setting up of a <a href="/wiki/501(c)(4)" class="mw-redirect" title="501(c)(4)">501(c)(4)</a> organization—<a href="/wiki/Colbert_Super_PAC" title="Colbert Super PAC">Colbert Super PAC</a>—as a way to "test the absurd limits of the Supreme Court's <a href="/wiki/Citizens_United_v._FEC" title="Citizens United v. FEC">Citizens United decision</a>"; Oliver's megachurch, in contrast, is a way to test whether the IRS might view his "megachurch" as a tax-exempt organization.<sup id="cite_ref-Mediaite_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mediaite-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> Steve Thorngate, writing in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Christian_Century" title="The Christian Century">The Christian Century</a></i>, suggested that the question of the religious exemption from taxation was more difficult and nuanced than Oliver portrayed, and not a simple matter of government regulation, describing Oliver's pivot to IRS policy as "unhelpful." However, Thorngate agreed that Oliver's exposure and criticism of "manipulative sleazeballs" who "fleece the faithful" is "spot-on."<sup id="cite_ref-ChristianCentury_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChristianCentury-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Leonardo Blair, writing for <i><a href="/wiki/Christian_Post" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian Post">Christian Post</a></i>, described Oliver's segment as a "brutal takedown" of televangelists and churches which preach "the <a href="/wiki/Prosperity_theology" title="Prosperity theology">prosperity gospel</a>," a message that dupes people into thinking that cash donations will solve medical or financial problems, while in fact the donations go to the personal aggrandizement of televangelists who buy expensive jets or large mansions.<sup id="cite_ref-ChristianPost_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChristianPost-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A week later, on the following episode, Oliver devoted a short segment to the donations the church had received, which included money from around the world. Oliver said he had received "thousands of envelopes with thousands of dollars" from donors. Displayed were several US Post Office containers full of mail. Oliver told viewers that the more money they sent in, the more "blessings" would be returned to them, adding that "that is still something I'm — amazingly — legally allowed to say."<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Oliver announced that the Church would be shutting down during his show on September 13, 2015. All monetary donations have been forwarded to Doctors Without Borders.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Donald_Trump">Donald Trump</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Last_Week_Tonight_with_John_Oliver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Donald Trump">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump_(Last_Week_Tonight)" title="Donald Trump (Last Week Tonight)">Donald Trump (Last Week Tonight)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Last_Week_Tonight_segments_about_Donald_Trump" title="Last Week Tonight segments about Donald Trump">Last Week Tonight segments about Donald Trump</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump_(Last_Week_Tonight)" title="Donald Trump (Last Week Tonight)">"Donald Trump"</a> is a segment discussing American businessman Donald Trump. It aired on February 28, 2016, as part of the third episode of the third season. During the 22-minute segment, Oliver discusses <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump_presidential_campaign,_2016" class="mw-redirect" title="Donald Trump presidential campaign, 2016">Trump's 2016 presidential campaign</a> and his long career in business. He also reveals that the Trump family name was changed at one point from the ancestral name 'Drumpf'. Although the changing of names was once a common <a href="/wiki/Anglicisation_of_names" title="Anglicisation of names">practice</a> among many non-English immigrants to the United States, the segment popularized the term "Donald Drumpf" and started a campaign urging viewers to "Make Donald Drumpf Again," a play on Trump's own campaign slogan, "<a href="/wiki/Make_America_Great_Again" title="Make America Great Again">Make America Great Again</a>." It is the most popular segment the show has ever done. The segment garnered more than 85 million views on Facebook and YouTube combined which, an HBO spokesman has said, "is a record for any piece of HBO content."<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> Throughout the <a href="/wiki/2016_United_States_presidential_election" title="2016 United States presidential election">2016 presidential election</a> and following <a href="/wiki/Inauguration_of_Donald_Trump" title="Inauguration of Donald Trump">Trump's inauguration</a>, Oliver made <a href="/wiki/Last_Week_Tonight_segments_about_Donald_Trump" title="Last Week Tonight segments about Donald Trump">additional segments regarding Trump</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Debt_buyers">Debt buyers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Last_Week_Tonight_with_John_Oliver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Debt buyers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>"Debt Buyers" is a segment discussing the business and questionable practice of <a href="/wiki/Debt_buyers" class="mw-redirect" title="Debt buyers">debt buyers</a>. It aired on June 5, 2016, as part of the fourteenth episode of the third season. Oliver announced he had purchased nearly $15 million in medical debt that belonged to 9,000 debtors. He did this through a company he had created, called "Central Asset Recovery Professionals Inc." (CARP), which he described as being "for <a href="/wiki/Carp" title="Carp">the bottom-feeding fish</a>." Oliver stated that it was "pretty clear by now [that] debt buying is a grimy business, and badly needs more oversight" and went to point out that starting such a business was "disturbingly easy."<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> It cost him $50 to register the business in <a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">Mississippi</a>, while it cost less than $60,000 to purchase almost $15 million in <a href="/wiki/Bad_debt" title="Bad debt">bad debt</a>, medical debt from Texas. Oliver forgave the debt in its entirety, and claimed that it was the largest single giveaway in the American television history, eclipsing that of <a href="/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">General Motors</a> on <i><a href="/wiki/The_Oprah_Winfrey_Show" title="The Oprah Winfrey Show">The Oprah Winfrey Show</a></i> in 2004 where it gifted cars to Winfrey's studio audience, worth an estimated $8 million in total.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Writing for <i>Slate</i>, Jordan Weissmann disputed the $15 million figure: "[Oliver] says CARP paid around $60,000 ... for its paper, which was 'out-of-statute'—meaning the debts were so old that creditors technically couldn't even sue over them anymore. That suggests the seller thought the debts were worth no more than, well, $60,000."<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> The episode was highlighted in the academic journal <i>Religions</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> The show partnered with RIP Medical Debt, Inc. to abolish the debt.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coal_mining_and_Bob_Murray">Coal mining and Bob Murray</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Last_Week_Tonight_with_John_Oliver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Coal mining and Bob Murray">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On June 18, 2017, <i>Last Week Tonight</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s main segment was about coal mining and <a href="/wiki/Robert_E._Murray" title="Robert E. Murray">Bob Murray</a>. In his segment, Oliver talked about how the safety condition inside the coal mines specifically the 2007 <a href="/wiki/Crandall_Canyon_Mine" title="Crandall Canyon Mine">Crandall Canyon Mine</a> collapse, which killed six miners, in which Oliver criticized Murray for claiming the collapse was caused by an <a href="/wiki/Earthquake" title="Earthquake">earthquake</a>, despite all evidence demonstrating otherwise.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> The show ended with a costumed <a href="/wiki/Squirrel" title="Squirrel">squirrel</a> named "Mr. Nutterbutter" presenting Murray with a voided check for "3 acorns and 18 cents" in response to Murray's company presenting its employees with low sums of money as bonuses, and how the employees retaliate by voiding the checks, and in reference to a claim that stated that Murray supposedly got the idea to start his company from a talking squirrel.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> On June 22, Bob Murray presented a <a href="/wiki/Lawsuit" title="Lawsuit">lawsuit</a> against Oliver, HBO, and <a href="/wiki/Time_Warner" class="mw-redirect" title="Time Warner">Time Warner</a> for defamation. HBO believes <i>Last Week Tonight</i> has done nothing wrong, with a <a href="/wiki/First_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="First Amendment to the United States Constitution">First Amendment</a> lawyer describing the lawsuit as "frivolous."<sup id="cite_ref-:0_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> In February 2018, a West Virginia judge stated that he plans to dismiss the lawsuit filed by Murray as unfounded.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After Murray’s appeal to the West Virginia Supreme Court was delayed due to impeachment of the judges, Murray offered to drop the charges and HBO accepted.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> On November 10, 2019, Oliver discussed the case in a segment about <a href="/wiki/SLAPP" class="mw-redirect" title="SLAPP">SLAPP suits</a>, revealing that the suit cost HBO $200,000 in legal costs and tripled the show’s libel insurance fees. Oliver acknowledged that, despite the segment being vetted, the content would likely lead to another lawsuit, and that he would stand behind his team if it were to happen.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> The segment was ended with a five-minute, <a href="/wiki/Times_Square" title="Times Square">Times Square</a>-set <a href="/wiki/Musical_number" class="mw-redirect" title="Musical number">musical number</a> featuring crude and ludicrous fictional anecdotes about Murray; the musical is titled "Eat Shit, Bob."<sup id="cite_ref-:1_126-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="A_Day_in_the_Life_of_Marlon_Bundo"><i>A Day in the Life of Marlon Bundo</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Last_Week_Tonight_with_John_Oliver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: A Day in the Life of Marlon Bundo">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/A_Day_in_the_Life_of_Marlon_Bundo" title="A Day in the Life of Marlon Bundo">A Day in the Life of Marlon Bundo</a></div>
<p>On March 18, 2018, Oliver announced the publication of a children's book, <i><a href="/wiki/A_Day_in_the_Life_of_Marlon_Bundo" title="A Day in the Life of Marlon Bundo">A Day in the Life of Marlon Bundo</a></i>, which parodies a book that <a href="/wiki/Mike_Pence" title="Mike Pence">Mike Pence</a>'s family wrote about their family rabbit, <a href="/wiki/Marlon_Bundo%27s:_A_Day_in_the_Life_of_the_Vice_President" class="mw-redirect" title="Marlon Bundo&#39;s: A Day in the Life of the Vice President">Marlon Bundo</a>. Oliver used his book as a platform to criticize Pence's positions on LGBT issues, as it featured a rabbit in a same-sex relationship.<sup id="cite_ref-Daily_Beast_Bundo_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Daily_Beast_Bundo-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> The book became the number-one book and e-book on <a href="/wiki/Amazon_(company)" title="Amazon (company)">Amazon</a> the next day, and the top-selling audiobook on <a href="/wiki/Audible_(store)" title="Audible (store)">Audible</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Politico_Bundo_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Politico_Bundo-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Russell_Crowe.27s_jockstrap"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Russell_Crowe's_jockstrap">Russell Crowe's jockstrap</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Last_Week_Tonight_with_John_Oliver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Russell Crowe&#039;s jockstrap">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Russell_Crowe%27s_jockstrap" title="Russell Crowe&#39;s jockstrap">Russell Crowe's jockstrap</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Russell_Crowe%27s_jockstrap" title="Russell Crowe&#39;s jockstrap">Russell Crowe's jockstrap</a> became a notable piece of memorabilia when it was purchased by the <i>Last Week Tonight with John Oliver</i> and then donated to a Blockbuster Video shop. The jockstrap reappeared in a skit in the final episode of the 2018 season of the show.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Robocalls">Robocalls</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Last_Week_Tonight_with_John_Oliver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Robocalls">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The March 10, 2019, episode dealt primarily with <a href="/wiki/Robocalls" class="mw-redirect" title="Robocalls">robocalls</a>, and how the problem had mushroomed in recent years since being increasingly deregulated under <a href="/wiki/Federal_Communications_Commission" title="Federal Communications Commission">FCC Chairman</a> <a href="/wiki/Ajit_Pai" title="Ajit Pai">Ajit Pai</a>. To prove the point of how easy it was for any company to begin flooding anyone with robocalls, Oliver had one of his IT techs develop a system in which all of the FCC chairs would, at regular intervals on their personal phone numbers, receive a robocall followed immediately by <a href="/wiki/Bagpipe" class="mw-redirect" title="Bagpipe">bagpipe</a> music. Oliver freely admitted his astonishment at just how easy this was to accomplish; the IT technician tasked with setting the system up told him they needed only 15 minutes to complete the project, doing so on a minimal budget. And per the very FCC regulations currently in place, Oliver quickly scrolled a section of fine print on screen at a random time during the segment, in which his office address was randomly embedded, and each FCC chair was required to send him an official letter to that address, notifying him in writing that they wished to have him cease all robocalls forthwith. Oliver was also flabbergasted at discovering that while tens of millions of dollars in fines were levied against companies for violating robocall regulations, only a few thousand dollars have ever been collected, effectively giving the companies free rein to continue robocalling unabated.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Guinness_World_Records"><i>Guinness World Records</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Last_Week_Tonight_with_John_Oliver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Guinness World Records">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The main story of the August 11, 2019 episode was <a href="/wiki/Turkmenistan" title="Turkmenistan">Turkmenistan</a> and its autocratic leader <a href="/wiki/Gurbanguly_Berdimuhamedow" title="Gurbanguly Berdimuhamedow">Gurbanguly Berdimuhamedow</a>. The last portion of the segment centered around Berdimuhamedow's obsession with <i><a href="/wiki/Guinness_World_Records" title="Guinness World Records">Guinness World Records</a></i>, with the Turkmen capital of <a href="/wiki/Ashgabat" title="Ashgabat">Ashgabat</a> being home to the most buildings with white marble cladding, the largest indoor <a href="/wiki/Ferris_wheel" title="Ferris wheel">Ferris wheel</a>, and the largest statue of a horse's head, among others. Oliver found that <i>Guinness World Records</i> received sums of money ranging from US$12,000 to US$500,000 from companies and authoritarian nations to set records for publicity. He ended the show with a 600 square foot <a href="/wiki/Marble_cake" title="Marble cake">marble cake</a> adorned with a picture of Berdimuhamedow falling from a horse; he had requested a Guinness adjudicator to certify it as the world's largest marble cake, a record previously set by <a href="/wiki/Betty_Crocker" title="Betty Crocker">Betty Crocker</a> in Saudi Arabia in 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> Guinness refused, stating that because their brand was "aligned with kids and families", they would not send an adjudicator; Oliver mockingly commented that he didn't "run a brutal enough dictatorship to meet Guinness World Records' high ethical standards". Oliver claimed that Guinness offered to certify it after the fact only if he signed an agreement not to criticize their practices on the show, which Oliver dismissed as "ridiculous".<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Guinness called Oliver's allegations "false and unfair", claiming that they did not send an adjudicator because they felt the cake was specifically for the purpose of mocking a record holder, stating it was their policy "not to partake in any activities which may belittle their achievements or subject them to ridicule".<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="International_broadcast">International broadcast</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Last_Week_Tonight_with_John_Oliver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: International broadcast">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><i>Last Week Tonight with John Oliver</i> episode clips can be seen internationally on <a href="/wiki/YouTube" title="YouTube">YouTube</a>. It is broadcast on <a href="/wiki/Pay_television" title="Pay television">premium</a> channel <a href="/wiki/HBO_Canada" class="mw-redirect" title="HBO Canada">HBO Canada</a> that is <a href="/wiki/Simulcast" title="Simulcast">simulcast</a> with HBO in the U.S.; around the same time it is shown on the international <a href="/wiki/HBO_(international)" title="HBO (international)">HBO</a> channels. It airs in Australia on <a href="/wiki/The_Comedy_Channel" title="The Comedy Channel">The Comedy Channel</a> hours after the U.S. airing<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> and airs in New Zealand on <a href="/wiki/SoHo_(TV_channel)" class="mw-redirect" title="SoHo (TV channel)">SoHo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> In the United Kingdom and Ireland, it is broadcast on Mondays on <a href="/wiki/Sky_Comedy" title="Sky Comedy">Sky Comedy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> In Belgium, it is broadcast on Thursdays by the <a href="/wiki/Telenet_(Belgium)" title="Telenet (Belgium)">Telenet</a> cable-only channel <a href="/wiki/Prime_(Flanders)" class="mw-redirect" title="Prime (Flanders)">PRIME Series</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> It airs in South Africa on <a href="/wiki/M-Net" title="M-Net">M-Net</a><sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> and in Portugal on <a href="/wiki/RTP3" title="RTP3">RTP3</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> In India, the show is broadcast on premium by <a href="/wiki/Hotstar" title="Hotstar">Hotstar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Last_Week_Tonight_with_John_Oliver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
